States state symbols">\n          Lists of United States state symbols\n         </a>\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n      </table>\n      <p>\n       <b>\n        Ohio\n       </b>\n       <span class="nowrap">\n        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">\n         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">\n          /\n          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">\n           <span title="/o\xca\x8a/: \'o\' in \'code\'">\n            o\xca\x8a\n           </span>\n           <span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\n            \xcb\x88\n           </span>\n           <span title="\'h\' in \'hi\'">\n            h\n           </span>\n           <span title="/a\xc9\xaa/: \'i\' in \'tide\'">\n            a\xc9\xaa\n           </span>\n           <span title="/./: syllable break">\n            .\n           </span>\n           <span title="/o\xca\x8a/: \'o\' in \'code\'">\n            o\xca\x8a\n           </span>\n          </span>\n          /\n         </a>\n        </span>\n        <small class="nowrap">\n         (\n         <span class="unicode haudio">\n          <span class="fn">\n           <span style="white-space:nowrap">\n            <a href="/wiki/File:En-us-Ohio.ogg" title="About this sound">\n             <img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/>\n            </a>\n           </span>\n           <a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e4/En-us-Ohio.ogg" title="En-us-Ohio.ogg">\n            listen\n           </a>\n          </span>\n         </span>\n         )\n        </small>\n       </span>\n       is a\n       <a href="/wiki/Midwestern_United_States" title="Midwestern United States">\n        Midwestern\n       </a>\n       state in the\n       <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes_region" title="Great Lakes region">\n        Great Lakes region\n       </a>\n       of the\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">\n        United States\n       </a>\n       . Ohio is the\n       <a href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._states_and_territories_by_area" title="List of U.S. states and territories by area">\n        34th largest by area\n       </a>\n       , the\n       <a href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._states_and_territories_by_population" title="List of U.S. states and territories by population">\n        7th most populous\n       </a>\n       , and the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._states_and_territories_by_population_density" title="List of U.S. states and territories by population density">\n        10th most densely populated\n       </a>\n       of the\n       <a href="/wiki/List_of_states_and_territories_of_the_United_States" title="List of states and territories of the United States">\n        50 United States\n       </a>\n       . The state\'s capital and\n       <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_in_Ohio" title="List of cities in Ohio">\n        largest city\n       </a>\n       is\n       <a href="/wiki/Columbus,_Ohio" title="Columbus, Ohio">\n        Columbus\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The state takes its name from the\n       <a href="/wiki/Ohio_River" title="Ohio River">\n        Ohio River\n       </a>\n       . The name originated from the\n       <a href="/wiki/Iroquoian_languages" title="Iroquoian languages">\n        Iroquois\n       </a>\n       language word\n       <i>\n        ohi-yo\n       </i>\n       , meaning "great river" or "large creek".\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">\n        <a href="#cite_note-21">\n         [21]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mithun312_22-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-mithun312-22">\n         [22]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">\n        <a href="#cite_note-23">\n         [23]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Partitioned from the\n       <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Territory" title="Northwest Territory">\n        Northwest Territory\n       </a>\n       , the state was admitted to the Union as the 17th state (and the first under the\n       <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Ordinance" title="Northwest Ordinance">\n        Northwest Ordinance\n       </a>\n       ) on March 1, 1803.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MS_10-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-MS-10">\n         [10]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WD_24-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-WD-24">\n         [24]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Ohio is historically known as the "Buckeye State" after its\n       <a href="/wiki/Aesculus_glabra" title="Aesculus glabra">\n        Ohio buckeye trees\n       </a>\n       , and Ohioans are also known as "Buckeyes".\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DNR_2-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-DNR-2">\n         [2]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The government of Ohio is composed of the executive branch, led by the\n       <a href="/wiki/List_of_Governors_of_Ohio" title="List of Governors of Ohio">\n        Governor\n       </a>\n       ; the legislative branch, which comprises the\n       <a href="/wiki/Ohio_General_Assembly" title="Ohio General Assembly">\n        Ohio General Assembly\n       </a>\n       ; and the judicial branch, which is led by the state\n       <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_Ohio" title="Supreme Court of Ohio">\n        Supreme Court\n       </a>\n       . Ohio occupies 16 seats in the\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">\n        United States House of Representatives\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">\n        <a href="#cite_note-25">\n         [25]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Ohio is known for its status as both a\n       <a href="/wiki/Swing_state" title="Swing state">\n        swing state\n       </a>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bell_26-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Bell-26">\n         [26]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and a\n       <a href="/wiki/Bellwether" title="Bellwether">\n        bellwether\n       </a>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bell_26-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Bell-26">\n         [26]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       in national elections. Six\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="Presidents of the United States">\n        Presidents of the United States\n       </a>\n       have been elected who had Ohio\n       <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States_by_home_state" title="List of Presidents of the United States by home state">\n        as their home state\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <p>\n      </p>\n      <div class="toc" id="toc">\n       <div class="toctitle">\n        <h2>\n         Contents\n        </h2>\n       </div>\n       <ul>\n        <li Americans\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-9">\n           <a href="#Colonial_and_Revolutionary_eras">\n            <span      <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-10">\n           <a href="#Northwest_Territory:_1787.E2.80.931803">\n            <span class="tocnumber">\n             4.3\n            </span>\n            <span class="toctext">\n             Northwest Territory: 1787\xe2\x80\x931803\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-11">\n           <a 1803\xe2\x80\x93present\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n         </ul>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-12">\n         <a href="#Demographics">\n Economy\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19">\n         <a href="#Transportation">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           7\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           Transportation\n          </span>\n         </a>\n         <ul>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20">\n           <a          <span class="tocnumber">\n             7.3\n            </span>\n            <span class="toctext">\n             Transportation lists\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n         </ul>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23">\n         <a href="#Law_and_government">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           8\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           Law and government\n          </span>\n         </a>\n         <ul>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-24">\n           <a href="#Executive_branch">\n            <span class="tocnumber">\n             8.1\n            </span>\n            <span class="toctext">\n             Executive branch\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-25">\n           <a href="#Judicial_branch">\n            <span class="tocnumber">\n             8.2\n            </span>\n            <span class="toctext">\n             Judicial branch\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-26">\n           <a href="#Legislative_branch">\n            <span class="tocnumber">\n             8.3\n            </span>\n            <span class="toctext">\n             Legislative branch\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-27">\n           <a href="#National_politics">\n            <span class="tocnumber">\n             8.4\n            </span>\n            <span class="toctext">\n             National politics\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n         </ul>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28">\n         <a href="#Education">\n Colleges and universities\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-30">\n           <a href="#Libraries">\n            <span class="tocnumber">\n             9.2\n            </span>\n            <span class="toctext">\n             Libraries\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n         </ul>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-31">\n         <a href="#Culture">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           10\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           Culture\n          </span>\n         </a>\n         <ul>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-32">\n           <a href="#Arts">\n            <span class="tocnumber">\n             10.1\n            </span>\n            <span class="toctext">\n             Arts\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-33">\n           <a         <span class="toctext">\n               Professional sports teams\n              </span>\n             </a>\n            </li>\n            <li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-35">\n             <a href="#Individual_sports">\n              <span class="tocnumber">\n               10.2.2\n              </span>\n              <span class="toctext">\n               Individual sports\n              </span>\n             </a>\n            </li>\n            <li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-36">\n             <a href="#College_sports">\n              <span class="tocnumber">\n               10.2.3\n              </span>\n              <span class="toctext">\n               College sports\n              </span>\n             </a>\n            </li>\n           </ul>\n          </li>\n class="toctext">\n           State symbols\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-38">\n         <a href="#See_also">\n          <span </a>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-40">\n         <a href="#References">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           14\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           References\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-41">\n         <a href="#External_links">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           15\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           External links\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </li>\n       </ul>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">\n        Etymology\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <p>\n       Ohio derives from the Iroquois language as their name for the Ohio River/ Alleghany River,\n       <i>\n        Ohi:yo\n       </i>\n       . This is pronounced\n       <i>\n        "Oh-hee-yoh,"\n       </i>\n       with the\n       <i>\n        i\n       </i>\n       sound being held an extra second. Folk etymology claims that this translates as "Beautiful River," however it appears that the word can be broken down as\n       <i>\n        "O-"\n       </i>\n       (pronoun prefix. Translates as "it" &amp; implies that whatever is about to follow it is considered a permanent condition of the item),\n       <i>\n        "Hih"\n       </i>\n       (verb. to spill) &amp;\n       <i>\n        "Gihedenyo"\n       </i>\n       (noun. creek, stream).\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">\n        <a href="#cite_note-27">\n         [27]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       That being said, the most sensible translation ought to be "Continuously-spilling Creek," or "Continuously-giving creek." The word for creek is used instead of river, since it still flows into a larger river, the Mississippi.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The common accent of Ohio shifts regularly, so there are multiple different accepted ways of saying Ohio that have been common throughout the last century, such as\n       <i>\n        "Oh-hai-yuh,"\n       </i>\n       <i>\n        "Uh-hai-yoh,"\n       </i>\n       &amp;\n       <i>\n        "Uh-hai-yuh."\n       </i>\n       The most recent shift that is beginning to surface sounds something along the lines of\n       <i>\n        "wh-hai-yuh."\n       </i>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">\n        Geography\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Further information:\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Ohio_counties" title="List of Ohio counties">\n        List of Ohio counties\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_in_Ohio" title="List of cities in Ohio">\n        List of cities in Ohio\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/List_of_villages_in_Ohio" title="List of villages in Ohio">\n        List of villages in Ohio\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Ohio_townships" title="List of Ohio townships">\n        List of Ohio townships\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ohio_public_lands" title="Ohio public lands">\n        Ohio public lands\n       </a>\n       , and\n       <a href="/wiki/List_of_lakes_in_Ohio" title="List of lakes in Ohio">\n        List of lakes in Ohio\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Ohio\'s geographic location has proven to be an asset for economic growth and expansion. Because Ohio links the Northeast to the Midwest, much cargo and business traffic passes through its borders along its well-developed highways. Ohio has the nation\'s 10th largest highway network, and is within a one-day drive of 50% of North America\'s population and 70% of North America\'s manufacturing capacity.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">\n        <a href="#cite_note-28">\n         [28]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       To the north,\n       <a href="/wiki/Lake_Erie" title="Lake Erie">\n        Lake Erie\n       </a>\n       gives Ohio 312 miles (502\xc2\xa0km) of coastline,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">\n        <a href="#cite_note-29">\n         [29]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       which allows for numerous cargo ports. Ohio\'s southern border is defined by the\n       <a href="/wiki/Ohio_River" title="Ohio River">\n        Ohio River\n       </a>\n       (with the border being at the 1792 low-water mark on the north side of the river),\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ohio_v_Kentucky_30-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Ohio_v_Kentucky-30">\n         [30]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and much of the northern border is defined by Lake Erie. Ohio\'s neighbors are\n       <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">\n        Pennsylvania\n       </a>\n       to the east,\n       <a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">\n        Michigan\n       </a>\n       to the northwest,\n       <a href="/wiki/Lake_Erie" title="Lake Erie">\n        Lake Erie\n       </a>\n       to the north,\n       <a href="/wiki/Indiana" title="Indiana">\n        Indiana\n       </a>\n       to the west,\n       <a href="/wiki/Kentucky" title="Kentucky">\n        Kentucky\n       </a>\n       on the south, and\n       <a href="/wiki/West_Virginia" title="West Virginia">\n        West Virginia\n       </a>\n       on the southeast. Ohio\'s borders were defined by\n       <a href="/wiki/Metes_and_bounds" title="Metes and bounds">\n        metes and bounds\n       </a>\n       in the\n       <a href="/wiki/Enabling_Act_of_1802" title="Enabling Act of 1802">\n        Enabling Act of 1802\n       </a>\n       as follows:\n      </p>\n      <blockquote class="templatequote">\n       <p>\n        Bounded on the east by the Pennsylvania line, on the south by the Ohio River, to the mouth of the\n        <a href="/wiki/Great_Miami_River" title="Great Miami River">\n         Great Miami River\n        </a>\n        , on the west by the line drawn due north from the mouth of the Great Miami aforesaid, and on the north by an east and west line drawn through the southerly extreme of\n        <a href="/wiki/Lake_Michigan" title="Lake Michigan">\n         Lake Michigan\n        </a>\n        , running east after intersecting the due north line aforesaid, from the mouth of the Great Miami until it shall intersect Lake Erie or the territorial line, and thence with the same through Lake Erie to the Pennsylvania line aforesaid.\n       </p>\n      </blockquote>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DSCN4516_portconneautflag_e.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="701" data-file-width="596" height="259" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/DSCN4516_portconneautflag_e.jpg/220px-DSCN4516_portconneautflag_e.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/DSCN4516_portconneautflag_e.jpg/330px-DSCN4516_portconneautflag_e.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/DSCN4516_portconneautflag_e.jpg/440px-DSCN4516_portconneautflag_e.jpg 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DSCN4516_portconneautflag_e.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         The Ohio coast of\n         <a href="/wiki/Lake_Erie" title="Lake Erie">\n          Lake Erie\n         </a>\n         .\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Ohio is bounded by the Ohio River, but nearly all of the river itself belongs to Kentucky and West Virginia. In 1980, the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Supreme_Court" title="U.S. Supreme Court">\n        U.S. Supreme Court\n       </a>\n       held that, based on the wording of the cessation of territory by\n       <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">\n        Virginia\n       </a>\n       (which at that time included what is now Kentucky and West Virginia), the boundary between Ohio and Kentucky (and, by implication, West Virginia) is the northern low-water mark of the river as it existed in 1792.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ohio_v_Kentucky_30-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Ohio_v_Kentucky-30">\n         [30]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Ohio has only that portion of the river between the river\'s 1792 low-water mark and the present high-water mark.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The border with Michigan has also changed, as a result of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Toledo_War" title="Toledo War">\n        Toledo War\n       </a>\n       , to angle slightly northeast to the north shore of the mouth of the Maumee River.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Much of Ohio features\n       <a href="/wiki/Glacial_till_plains_(Ohio)" title="Glacial till plains (Ohio)">\n        glaciated till plains\n       </a>\n       , with an exceptionally flat area in the northwest being known as the\n       <a href="/wiki/Great_Black_Swamp" title="Great Black Swamp">\n        Great Black Swamp\n       </a>\n       . This glaciated region in the northwest and central state is bordered to the east and southeast first by a belt known as the\n       <a href="/wiki/Glaciated_Allegheny_Plateau" title="Glaciated Allegheny Plateau">\n        glaciated Allegheny Plateau\n       </a>\n       , and then by another belt known as the\n       <a href="/wiki/Unglaciated_Allegheny_Plateau" title="Unglaciated Allegheny Plateau">\n        unglaciated Allegheny Plateau\n       </a>\n       . Most of Ohio is of low relief, but the unglaciated Allegheny Plateau\n       <a href="/wiki/Appalachian_Ohio" title="Appalachian Ohio">\n        features rugged hills and forests\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <div class="thumb tleft">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Geographic_regions_ohio.svg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="870" data-file-width="650" height="294" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Geographic_regions_ohio.svg/220px-Geographic_regions_ohio.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Geographic_regions_ohio.svg/330px-Geographic_regions_ohio.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Geographic_regions_ohio.svg/440px-Geographic_regions_ohio.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Geographic_regions_ohio.svg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Physical geography of Ohio.\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       The rugged southeastern quadrant of Ohio, stretching in an outward bow-like arc along the Ohio River from the West\n       <a href="/wiki/Northern_Panhandle_of_West_Virginia" title="Northern Panhandle of West Virginia">\n        Virginia Panhandle\n       </a>\n       to the outskirts of Cincinnati, forms a distinct\n       <a href="/wiki/Socioeconomics" title="Socioeconomics">\n        socio-economic\n       </a>\n       unit. Geologically similar to parts of West Virginia and southwestern Pennsylvania, this area\'s coal mining legacy, dependence on small pockets of old manufacturing establishments, and distinctive regional dialect set this section off from the rest of the state. In 1965 the\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">\n        United States Congress\n       </a>\n       passed the Appalachian Regional Development Act, at attempt to "address the persistent poverty and growing economic despair of the Appalachian Region."\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">\n        <a href="#cite_note-31">\n         [31]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       This act defines 29 Ohio counties as part of Appalachia.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">\n        <a href="#cite_note-32">\n         [32]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       While 1/3 of Ohio\'s land mass is part of the federally defined Appalachian region, only 12.8% of Ohioans live there (1.476 million people.)\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">\n        <a href="#cite_note-33">\n         [33]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:217px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Ohio_NA.png">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1333" data-file-width="1967" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Map_of_Ohio_NA.png/215px-Map_of_Ohio_NA.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Map_of_Ohio_NA.png/323px-Map_of_Ohio_NA.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Map_of_Ohio_NA.png/430px-Map_of_Ohio_NA.png 2x" width="215"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Ohio_NA.png" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Map of Ohio.\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Significant\n       <a href="/wiki/List_of_rivers_of_Ohio" title="List of rivers of Ohio">\n        rivers\n       </a>\n       within the state include the\n       <a href="/wiki/Cuyahoga_River" title="Cuyahoga River">\n        Cuyahoga River\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Great_Miami_River" title="Great Miami River">\n        Great Miami River\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Maumee_River" title="Maumee River">\n        Maumee River\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Muskingum_River" title="Muskingum River">\n        Muskingum River\n       </a>\n       , and\n       <a href="/wiki/Scioto_River" title="Scioto River">\n        Scioto River\n       </a>\n       . The rivers in the northern part of the state drain into the northern Atlantic Ocean via\n       <a href="/wiki/Lake_Erie" title="Lake Erie">\n        Lake Erie\n       </a>\n       and the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Lawrence_River" title="St. Lawrence River">\n        St. Lawrence River\n       </a>\n       , and the rivers in the southern part of the state drain into the\n       <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">\n        Gulf of Mexico\n       </a>\n       via the\n       <a href="/wiki/Ohio_River" title="Ohio River">\n        Ohio River\n       </a>\n       and then the\n       <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">\n        Mississippi\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The worst weather disaster in Ohio history occurred along the Great Miami River in 1913. Known as the\n       <a href="/wiki/Great_Dayton_Flood" title="Great Dayton Flood">\n        Great Dayton Flood\n       </a>\n       , the entire\n       <a href="/wiki/Great_Miami_River" title="Great Miami River">\n        Miami River\n       </a>\n       watershed flooded, including the downtown business district of\n       <a href="/wiki/Dayton,_Ohio" title="Dayton, Ohio">\n        Dayton\n       </a>\n       . As a result, the\n       <a href="/wiki/Miami_Conservancy_District" title="Miami Conservancy District">\n        Miami Conservancy District\n       </a>\n       was created as the first major flood plain engineering project in Ohio and the United States.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MCDAct_34-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-MCDAct-34">\n         [34]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grand_Lake_St._Marys" title="Grand Lake St. Marys">\n        Grand Lake St. Marys\n       </a>\n       in the west central part of the state was constructed as a supply of water for\n       <a href="/wiki/Canal" title="Canal">\n        canals\n       </a>\n       in the canal-building era of 1820\xe2\x80\x931850. For many years this body of water, over 20 square miles (52\xc2\xa0km\n       <sup>\n        2\n       </sup>\n       ), was the largest artificial lake in the world. It should be noted that\n       <a href="/wiki/Category:Canals_in_Ohio" title="Category:Canals in Ohio">\n        Ohio\'s canal-building projects\n       </a>\n       were not the economic fiasco that similar efforts were in other states. Some cities, such as Dayton, owe their industrial emergence to location on canals, and as late as 1910 interior canals carried much of the bulk freight of the state.\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">\n        Climate\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:OH_koppen.svg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1350" data-file-width="945" height="429" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/OH_koppen.svg/300px-OH_koppen.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/OH_koppen.svg/450px-OH_koppen.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/OH_koppen.svg/600px-OH_koppen.svg.png 2x" width="300"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:OH_koppen.svg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         K\xc3\xb6ppen climate types in Ohio\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       The climate of Ohio is a\n       <a href="/wiki/Humid_continental_climate" title="Humid continental climate">\n        humid continental climate\n       </a>\n       (\n       <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="K\xc3\xb6ppen climate classification">\n        K\xc3\xb6ppen climate classification\n       </a>\n       <i>\n        Dfa/Dfb\n       </i>\n       ) throughout most of the state except in the extreme southern counties of Ohio\'s\n       <a href="/wiki/Bluegrass_region" title="Bluegrass region">\n        Bluegrass region\n       </a>\n       section which are located on the northern periphery of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Humid_subtropical_climate" title="Humid subtropical climate">\n        humid subtropical climate\n       </a>\n       (\n       <i>\n        Cfa\n       </i>\n       ) and\n       <a href="/wiki/Upland_South" title="Upland South">\n        Upland South\n       </a>\n       region of the United States. Summers are typically hot and humid throughout the state, while winters generally range from cool to cold. Precipitation in Ohio is moderate year-round. Severe weather is not uncommon in the state, although there are typically fewer\n       <a href="/wiki/Tornado" title="Tornado">\n        tornado\n       </a>\n       reports in Ohio than in states located in what is known as the\n       <a href="/wiki/Tornado_Alley" title="Tornado Alley">\n        Tornado Alley\n       </a>\n       . Severe\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lake_effect_snow" title="Lake effect snow">\n        lake effect snowstorms\n       </a>\n       are also not uncommon on the southeast shore of\n       <a href="/wiki/Lake_Erie" title="Lake Erie">\n        Lake Erie\n       </a>\n       , which is located in an area designated as the\n       <a href="/wiki/Snowbelt" title="Snowbelt">\n        Snowbelt\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Although predominantly not in a subtropical climate, some warmer-climate flora and fauna does reach well into Ohio. For instance, a number of trees with more southern ranges, such as the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blackjack_oak" title="Blackjack oak">\n        blackjack oak\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <i>\n        Quercus marilandica\n       </i>\n       , are found at their northernmost in Ohio just north of the Ohio River. Also evidencing this climatic transition from a\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subtropical" title="Subtropical">\n        subtropical\n       </a>\n       to\n       <a href="/wiki/Continental_climate" title="Continental climate">\n        continental climate\n       </a>\n       , several plants such as the Southern magnolia\n       <i>\n        (\n        <a href="/wiki/Magnolia_grandiflora" title="Magnolia grandiflora">\n         Magnolia grandiflora\n        </a>\n        )\n       </i>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Albizia_julibrissin" title="Albizia julibrissin">\n        Albizia julibrissin\n       </a>\n       (mimosa),\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crape_Myrtle" title="Crape Myrtle">\n        Crape Myrtle\n       </a>\n       , and even the occasional\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Needle_Palm" title="Needle Palm">\n        Needle Palm\n       </a>\n       are hardy landscape materials regularly used as street, yard, and garden plantings in the\n       <a href="/wiki/Bluegrass_region" title="Bluegrass region">\n        Bluegrass region\n       </a>\n       of Ohio; but these same plants will simply not thrive in much of the rest of the State. This interesting change may be observed while traveling through Ohio on\n       <a href="/wiki/Interstate_75" title="Interstate 75">\n        Interstate 75\n       </a>\n       from\n       <a href="/wiki/Cincinnati" title="Cincinnati">\n        Cincinnati\n       </a>\n       to\n       <a href="/wiki/Toledo,_Ohio" title="Toledo, Ohio">\n        Toledo\n       </a>\n       ; the observant traveler of this diverse state may even catch a glimpse of Cincinnati\'s\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Common_wall_lizard" title="Common wall lizard">\n        common wall lizard\n       </a>\n       , one of the few examples of permanent "subtropical" fauna in Ohio.\n      </p>\n      <table class="wikitable sortable" style="margin:auto;">\n       <caption>\n        Average daily maximum and minimum temperatures for selected cities in Ohio\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ohio_climate_averages_35-0">\n         <a href="#cite_note-Ohio_climate_averages-35">\n <td>\n         0/\xe2\x80\x937\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <td>\n         <a href="/wiki/Dayton,_Ohio" title="Dayton, Ohio">\n          Dayton\n         </a>\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         87/67\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         31/19\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         36/22\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         2/\xe2\x80\x935\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <td>\n         <a href="/wiki/Canton,_Ohio" title="Canton, Ohio">\n          Canton\n         </a>\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         82/62\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         28/16\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         33/19\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         1/\xe2\x80\x937\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n      </table>\n      <h4>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Records">\n        Records\n       </span>\n      </h4>\n      <p>\n       The highest recorded temperature was 113\n       <a href="/wiki/Fahrenheit" title="Fahrenheit">\n        \xc2\xb0F\n       </a>\n       (45\n       <a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">\n        \xc2\xb0C\n       </a>\n       ), near\n       <a href="/wiki/Gallipolis,_Ohio" title="Gallipolis, Ohio">\n        Gallipolis\n       </a>\n       on July 21, 1934.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">\n        <a href="#cite_note-36">\n         [36]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The lowest recorded temperature was \xe2\x88\x9239\xc2\xa0\xc2\xb0F (\xe2\x88\x9239\xc2\xa0\xc2\xb0C), at\n       <a href="/wiki/Milligan,_Ohio" title="Milligan, Ohio">\n        Milligan\n       </a>\n       on February 10, 1899,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">\n        <a href="#cite_note-37">\n         [37]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       during the\n       <a href="/wiki/Great_Blizzard_of_1899" title="Great Blizzard of 1899">\n        Great Blizzard of 1899\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">\n        <a href="#cite_note-38">\n         [38]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Earthquakes">\n        Earthquakes\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       Although few have registered as noticeable to the average resident, more than 30 earthquakes occurred in Ohio between 2002 and 2007, and more than 200 quakes with a\n       <a href="/wiki/Richter_magnitude_scale" title="Richter magnitude scale">\n        magnitude\n       </a>\n       of 2.0 or higher have occurred since 1776.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">\n        <a href="#cite_note-39">\n         [39]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The most substantial known earthquake in Ohio history was the\n       <a href="/wiki/Anna,_Ohio" title="Anna, Ohio">\n        Anna\n       </a>\n       (Shelby County) earthquake,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">\n        <a href="#cite_note-40">\n         [40]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       which occurred on March 9, 1937. It was centered in western Ohio, and had a magnitude of 5.4, and was of\n       <a href="/wiki/Mercalli_intensity_scale" title="Mercalli intensity scale">\n        intensity\n       </a>\n       VIII.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">\n        <a href="#cite_note-41">\n         [41]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Other significant earthquakes in Ohio include:\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">\n        <a href="#cite_note-42">\n         [42]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       one of magnitude 4.8 near\n       <a href="/wiki/Lima,_Ohio" title="Lima, Ohio">\n        Lima\n       </a>\n       on September 19, 1884;\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">\n        <a href="#cite_note-43">\n         [43]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       one of magnitude 4.2 near\n       <a href="/wiki/Portsmouth,_Ohio" title="Portsmouth, Ohio">\n        Portsmouth\n       </a>\n       on May 17, 1901;\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">\n        <a href="#cite_note-44">\n         [44]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and one of 5.0 in\n       <a href="/wiki/LeRoy_Township,_Lake_County,_Ohio" title="LeRoy Township, Lake County, Ohio">\n        LeRoy Township in Lake County\n       </a>\n       on January 31, 1986, which continued to trigger 13 aftershocks of magnitude 0.5 to 2.4 for two months.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">\n        <a href="#cite_note-45">\n         [45]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">\n        <a href="#cite_note-46">\n         [46]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The most recent earthquake in Ohio of any appreciable magnitude occurred on December 31, 2011, at 3:05pm\n       <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Time_Zone" title="Eastern Time Zone">\n        EST\n       </a>\n       . It had a magnitude of 4.0, and its epicenter was located approximately 4 kilometres northwest of\n       <a href="/wiki/Youngstown,_Ohio" title="Youngstown, Ohio">\n        Youngstown\n       </a>\n       (\n       <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion">\n        <a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Ohio&amp;params=41_7_19.1994_N_80_41_2.3994_W_">\n         <span class="geo-default">\n          <span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">\n           <span class="latitude">\n            41\xc2\xb07\xe2\x80\xb219.1994\xe2\x80\xb3N\n           </span>\n           <span class="longitude">\n            80\xc2\xb041\xe2\x80\xb22.3994\xe2\x80\xb3W\n           </span>\n          </span>\n         </span>\n         <span class="geo-multi-punct">\n          \xef\xbb\xbf / \xef\xbb\xbf\n         </span>\n         <span class="geo-nondefault">\n          <span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">\n           41.121999833\xc2\xb0N 80.683999833\xc2\xb0W\n          </span>\n          <span style="display:none">\n           \xef\xbb\xbf /\n           <span class="geo">\n            41.121999833; -80.683999833\n           </span>\n          </span>\n         </span>\n        </a>\n       </span>\n       ), near the\n       <a href="/wiki/Trumbull_County,_Ohio" title="Trumbull County, Ohio">\n        Trumbull\n       </a>\n       /\n       <a href="/wiki/Mahoning_County,_Ohio" title="Mahoning County, Ohio">\n        Mahoning\n       </a>\n       county border.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">\n        <a href="#cite_note-47">\n         [47]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The Ohio Seismic Network (OhioSeis), a group of seismograph stations at several colleges, universities, and other institutions, and coordinated by the Division of Geological Survey of the Ohio Department of Natural Resources,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">\n        <a href="#cite_note-48">\n         [48]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       maintains an extensive catalog of Ohio earthquakes from 1776 to the present day, as well as earthquakes located in other states whose effects were felt in Ohio.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">\n        <a href="#cite_note-49">\n         [49]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Major_cities">\n        Major cities\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n </span>\n      </h2>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/History_of_Ohio" title="History of Ohio">\n        History of Ohio\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Native_Americans">\n        Native Americans\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       Archeological evidence suggests that the Ohio Valley was inhabited by\n       <a href="/wiki/Nomad" title="Nomad">\n        nomadic people\n       </a>\n       as early as 13,000\xc2\xa0BC.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-knepper9_51-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-knepper9-51">\n         [51]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       These early nomads disappeared from Ohio by 1,000\xc2\xa0BC, "but their\n       <a href="/wiki/Archaeological_culture" title="Archaeological culture">\n        material culture\n       </a>\n       provided a base for those who followed them".\n       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">\n        [\n        <i>\n         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Attribution_needed" title="Wikipedia:Attribution needed">\n          <span title="This sentence may contain perspectives and opinions without stating whose they are. (October 2016)">\n           attribution needed\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </i>\n        ]\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-knepper9_51-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-knepper9-51">\n         [51]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Between 1,000 and 800\xc2\xa0BC, the sedentary\n       <a href="/wiki/Adena_culture" title="Adena culture">\n        Adena culture\n       </a>\n       emerged. As Ohio historian George W. Knepper notes, this sophisticated culture was "so named because evidences of their culture were excavated in 1902 on the grounds of Adena,\n       <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Worthington_(governor)" title="Thomas Worthington (governor)">\n        Thomas Worthington\n       </a>\n       \'s estate located near\n       <a href="/wiki/Chillicothe,_Ohio" title="Chillicothe, Ohio">\n        Chillicothe\n       </a>\n       ".\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-knepper10_52-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-knepper10-52">\n         [52]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The Adena were able to establish "semi-permanent" villages because they domesticated plants, which included\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Squash_(plant)" title="Squash (plant)">\n        squash\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunflowers" title="Sunflowers">\n        sunflowers\n       </a>\n       , and perhaps\n       <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">\n        corn\n       </a>\n       . Cultivation of these in addition to hunting and gathering supported more settled, complex villages.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-knepper10_52-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-knepper10-52">\n         [52]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The most spectacular remnant of the Adena culture is the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Serpent_Mound" title="Great Serpent Mound">\n        Great Serpent Mound\n       </a>\n       , located in\n       <a href="/wiki/Adams_County,_Ohio" title="Adams County, Ohio">\n        Adams County, Ohio\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-knepper10_52-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-knepper10-52">\n         [52]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:212px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:5NationsExpansion.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="443" data-file-width="441" height="211" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/5NationsExpansion.jpg/210px-5NationsExpansion.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/5NationsExpansion.jpg/315px-5NationsExpansion.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/5NationsExpansion.jpg/420px-5NationsExpansion.jpg 2x" width="210"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:5NationsExpansion.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         <a href="/wiki/Iroquois" title="Iroquois">\n          Iroquois\n         </a>\n         conquests during the\n         <a href="/wiki/Beaver_Wars" title="Beaver Wars">\n          Beaver Wars\n         </a>\n         (mid-1600s), which largely depopulated the upper and mid-\n         <a href="/wiki/Ohio_River" title="Ohio River">\n          Ohio River\n         </a>\n         valley.\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Around 100\xc2\xa0BC, the Adena evolved into the\n       <a href="/wiki/Hopewell_tradition" title="Hopewell tradition">\n        Hopewell\n       </a>\n       people, who were named for the farm owned by Captain M. C. Hopewell, where evidence of their unique culture was discovered.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-knepper11_53-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-knepper11-53">\n         [53]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Like the Adena, the Hopewell people participated in a mound-building culture. Their complex, large and technologically sophisticated\n       <a href="/wiki/Earthworks_(archaeology)" title="Earthworks (archaeology)">\n        earthworks\n       </a>\n       can be found in modern-day\n       <a href="/wiki/Marietta,_Ohio" title="Marietta, Ohio">\n        Marietta\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Newark,_Ohio" title="Newark, Ohio">\n        Newark\n       </a>\n       , and\n       <a href="/wiki/Circleville,_Ohio" title="Circleville, Ohio">\n        Circleville\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-knepper11_53-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-knepper11-53">\n         [53]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       They were also a powerful trading society, managing to knit together a network that passed goods throughout a third of the continent.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">\n        <a href="#cite_note-54">\n         [54]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The Hopewell, however, disappeared from the Ohio Valley in about 600\xc2\xa0AD. Little is known about the people who replaced them, although many Siouan-speaking peoples from the Plains &amp; East Coast claim them as ancestors &amp; say they lived throughout the Ohio region until approx. the 13th century.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-knepper13_55-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-knepper13-55">\n         [55]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       It is possible that the rise of the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mississippian_Culture" title="Mississippian Culture">\n        Mississippian Culture\n       </a>\n       was the downfall of the Hopewell, as they began to rise to prominence on the Mississippi River around the same time that the Hopewell Culture died out.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Researchers have identified three additional, distinct prehistoric cultures: the\n       <a href="/wiki/Fort_Ancient" title="Fort Ancient">\n        Fort Ancient\n       </a>\n       people, the Whittlesey Focus people\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-knepper13_55-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-knepper13-55">\n         [55]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       &amp; the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monongahela_Culture" title="Monongahela Culture">\n        Monongahela Culture\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">\n        <a href="#cite_note-56">\n         [56]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       All three cultures apparently disappeared in the 17th century, perhaps decimated by infectious diseases spread in epidemics from early European contact. The Native Americans had no immunity to common European diseases. No one has ever definitively concluded which historically known peoples they may have been analogous to. That being said, it is generally believed that the Shawnees may have absorbed the Fort Ancient people.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-knepper13_55-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-knepper13-55">\n         [55]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       It\'s also possible that the Monongahela held no land in Ohio during by the Colonial Era. The Mississippian Culture were close to, contemporaneous with, and traded extensively with the Fort Ancient people.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       American Indians in the Ohio Valley were greatly affected by the aggressive tactics of the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iroquois_Confederation" title="Iroquois Confederation">\n        Iroquois Confederation\n       </a>\n       , based in central and western New York.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-knepper14_57-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-knepper14-57">\n         [57]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       After the\n       <a href="/wiki/Beaver_Wars" title="Beaver Wars">\n        Beaver Wars\n       </a>\n       in the mid-17th century, the Iroquois claimed much of the Ohio country as hunting and, more importantly, beaver-trapping ground. After the devastation of epidemics and war in the mid-17th century, which largely emptied the Ohio country of indigenous people by the mid-to-late 17th century, the land gradually became repopulated by the mostly\n       <a href="/wiki/Algonquian_languages" title="Algonquian languages">\n        Algonquian\n       </a>\n       . Many of these Ohio-country nations were multi-ethnic (sometimes multi-linguistic) societies born out of the earlier devastation brought about by disease, war, and subsequent social instability. They subsisted on agriculture (\n       <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">\n        corn\n       </a>\n       , sunflowers,\n       <a href="/wiki/Bean" title="Bean">\n        beans\n       </a>\n       , etc.) supplemented by seasonal hunts. By the 18th century, they were part of a larger global economy brought about by European entry into the\n       <a href="/wiki/Fur_trade" title="Fur trade">\n        fur trade\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-roseboom20_58-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-roseboom20-58">\n         [58]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The indigenous nations to inhabit Ohio in the historical period included the Iroquoian\n       <a href="/wiki/Petun" title="Petun">\n        Petun\n       </a>\n       (known for their Tobacco plantations),\n       <a href="/wiki/Erie_people" title="Erie people">\n        Erie\n       </a>\n       (thought to have been from Northeast Ohio &amp; western Pennsylvania, but may have come from Canada),\n       <a href="/wiki/Neutral_Nation" title="Neutral Nation">\n        Chonnonton\n       </a>\n       (Conquered their way down from Canada during Beaver Wars before being defeated by the Iroquois Confederacy &amp; their allies),\n       <a href="/wiki/Wyandot_people" title="Wyandot people">\n        Wyandot\n       </a>\n       (a group of Petun who became isolated around Cleveland after the Beaver Wars. Commonly mistaken for the Huron, whom most surviving Petun later joined),\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">\n        <a href="#cite_note-59">\n         [59]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       the\n       <a href="/wiki/Mingo" title="Mingo">\n        Mingo Seneca\n       </a>\n       (split off from the Iroquois Confederacy &amp; moved to Ohio in 18th century. Remained approx. 100 years.) &amp; the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iroquois_Confederacy" title="Iroquois Confederacy">\n        Iroquois Confederacy\n       </a>\n       (conquered most of Ohio at the bequest of the English in the 1660s. Pushed back to Pennsylvania by French in 1701.), The Algonquian\n       <a href="/wiki/Miami_people" title="Miami people">\n        Miami\n       </a>\n       (Mostly from Indiana.),\n       <a href="/wiki/Mascouten" title="Mascouten">\n        Mascouten\n       </a>\n       (close sister tribe to Miamis. Scattered during Beaver Wars. Mostly relocated to Kentucky)\n       <a href="/wiki/Lenape" title="Lenape">\n        Lenape\n       </a>\n       (Arrived around the turn of the 18th century from east coast),\n       <a href="/wiki/Shawnee" title="Shawnee">\n        Shawnee\n       </a>\n       (Seceded from Powhatan Confederacy. Eventually came to Ohio River &amp; most likely merged with several other lesser known people in the region) &amp;\n       <a href="/wiki/Odawa" title="Odawa">\n        Odawa\n       </a>\n       (part of a Confederacy that surrounded Lake Superior. Relocated to Michigan &amp; Northwest Ohio around the American Revolution.) &amp; the Siouan\n       <a href="/wiki/Mosopelea" title="Mosopelea">\n        Mosopelea\n       </a>\n       (moved to Arkansas during Beaver Wars).\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">\n        <a href="#cite_note-60">\n         [60]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-knepper14-17_61-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-knepper14-17-61">\n         [61]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ohio_country" title="Ohio country">\n        Ohio country\n       </a>\n       was also the site of Indian massacres, such as the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chief_Logan#Yellow_Creek_Massacre" title="Chief Logan">\n        Yellow Creek Massacre\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Gnadenhutten_massacre" title="Gnadenhutten massacre">\n        Gnadenhutten\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pontiac%27s_Rebellion_school_massacre" title="Pontiac\'s Rebellion school massacre">\n        Pontiac\'s Rebellion school massacre\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-knepper43-44_62-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-knepper43-44-62">\n         [62]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Most Native Peoples who remained in Ohio were slowly bought out and convinced to leave, or ordered to do so by law, in the early 19th century with the\n       <a href="/wiki/Indian_Removal_Act" title="Indian Removal Act">\n        Indian Removal Act\n       </a>\n       of 1830.\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Colonial_and_Revolutionary_eras">\n        Colonial and Revolutionary eras\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       During the 18th century, the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_colonisation_of_the_Americas" title="French colonisation of the Americas">\n        French\n       </a>\n       set up a system of\n       <a href="/wiki/Trading_post" title="Trading post">\n        trading posts\n       </a>\n       to control the fur trade in the region. Beginning in 1754, France and\n       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">\n        Great Britain\n       </a>\n       fought a war that was known in North America as the\n       <a href="/wiki/French_and_Indian_War" title="French and Indian War">\n        French and Indian War\n       </a>\n       and in Europe as the\n       <a href="/wiki/Seven_Years%27_War" title="Seven Years\' War">\n        Seven Years\' War\n       </a>\n       . As a result of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1763)" title="Treaty of Paris (1763)">\n        Treaty of Paris\n       </a>\n       , the French ceded control of Ohio and the remainder of the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Northwest" title="Old Northwest">\n        Old Northwest\n       </a>\n       to Great Britain.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pontiac%27s_Rebellion" title="Pontiac\'s Rebellion">\n        Pontiac\'s Rebellion\n       </a>\n       in the 1760s, however, posed a challenge to British military control.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">\n        <a href="#cite_note-63">\n         [63]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       This came to an end with the colonists\' victory in the\n       <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">\n        American Revolution\n       </a>\n       . In the\n       <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1783)" title="Treaty of Paris (1783)">\n        Treaty of Paris\n       </a>\n       in 1783, Britain ceded all claims to Ohio country to the United States.\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span id="Northwest_Territory:_1787\xe2\x80\x931803">\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Northwest_Territory:_1787.E2.80.931803">\n        Northwest Territory: 1787\xe2\x80\x931803\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DSCN3504_ohiocompany_e.JPG">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="573" data-file-width="600" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/DSCN3504_ohiocompany_e.JPG/220px-DSCN3504_ohiocompany_e.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/DSCN3504_ohiocompany_e.JPG/330px-DSCN3504_ohiocompany_e.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/DSCN3504_ohiocompany_e.JPG/440px-DSCN3504_ohiocompany_e.JPG 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DSCN3504_ohiocompany_e.JPG" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Plaque commemorating the\n         <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Ordinance" title="Northwest Ordinance">\n          Northwest Ordinance\n         </a>\n         outside\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Federal_Hall_National_Memorial" title="Federal Hall National Memorial">\n          Federal Hall National Memorial\n         </a>\n         in New York\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       The United States created the\n       <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Territory" title="Northwest Territory">\n        Northwest Territory\n       </a>\n       under the\n       <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Ordinance" title="Northwest Ordinance">\n        Northwest Ordinance\n       </a>\n       of 1787.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cayton3_64-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-cayton3-64">\n         [64]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Slavery was not permitted in the new territory. Settlement began with the founding of\n       <a href="/wiki/Marietta,_Ohio" title="Marietta, Ohio">\n        Marietta\n       </a>\n       by the\n       <a href="/wiki/Ohio_Company_of_Associates" title="Ohio Company of Associates">\n        Ohio Company of Associates\n       </a>\n       , which had been formed by a group of American Revolutionary War veterans. Following the Ohio Company, the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miami_Purchase" title="Miami Purchase">\n        Miami Company\n       </a>\n       (also referred to as the "\n       <a href="/wiki/Symmes_Purchase" title="Symmes Purchase">\n        Symmes Purchase\n       </a>\n       ") claimed the southwestern section, and the\n       <a href="/wiki/Connecticut_Land_Company" title="Connecticut Land Company">\n        Connecticut Land Company\n       </a>\n       surveyed and settled the\n       <a href="/wiki/Connecticut_Western_Reserve" title="Connecticut Western Reserve">\n        Connecticut Western Reserve\n       </a>\n       in present-day\n       <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Ohio" title="Northeast Ohio">\n        Northeast Ohio\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The old Northwest Territory originally included areas previously known as\n       <a href="/wiki/Ohio_Country" title="Ohio Country">\n        Ohio Country\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Illinois_Country" title="Illinois Country">\n        Illinois Country\n       </a>\n       . As Ohio prepared for statehood, the\n       <a href="/wiki/Indiana_Territory" title="Indiana Territory">\n        Indiana Territory\n       </a>\n       was created, reducing the Northwest Territory to approximately the size of present-day Ohio plus the eastern half of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Lower_Peninsula_of_Michigan" title="Lower Peninsula of Michigan">\n        Lower Peninsula of Michigan\n       </a>\n       and the eastern tip of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Upper_Peninsula_of_Michigan" title="Upper Peninsula of Michigan">\n        Upper Peninsula\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Under the\n       <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Ordinance" title="Northwest Ordinance">\n        Northwest Ordinance\n       </a>\n       , areas of the territory could be defined and admitted as states once their population reached 60,000. Although Ohio\'s population numbered only 45,000 in December 1801,\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">\n        Congress\n       </a>\n       determined that the population was growing rapidly and Ohio could begin the path to statehood. The assumption was that it would exceed 60,000 residents by the time it was admitted as a state. Furthermore, in regards to the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leni_Lenape" title="Leni Lenape">\n        Leni Lenape\n       </a>\n       Native Americans living in the region, Congress decided that 10,000 acres on the\n       <a href="/wiki/Muskingum_River" title="Muskingum River">\n        Muskingum River\n       </a>\n       in the present state of Ohio would "be set apart and the property thereof be vested in the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moravian_Brethren" title="Moravian Brethren">\n        Moravian Brethren\n       </a>\n       ... or a society of the said Brethren for civilizing the Indians and promoting Christianity".\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">\n        <a href="#cite_note-65">\n         [65]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span id="Statehood:_1803\xe2\x80\x93present">\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Statehood:_1803.E2.80.93present">\n        Statehood: 1803\xe2\x80\x93present\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:James_Abram_Garfield,_photo_portrait_seated.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4034" data-file-width="3182" height="279" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/James_Abram_Garfield%2C_photo_portrait_seated.jpg/220px-James_Abram_Garfield%2C_photo_portrait_seated.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/James_Abram_Garfield%2C_photo_portrait_seated.jpg/330px-James_Abram_Garfield%2C_photo_portrait_seated.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/James_Abram_Garfield%2C_photo_portrait_seated.jpg/440px-James_Abram_Garfield%2C_photo_portrait_seated.jpg 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:James_Abram_Garfield,_photo_portrait_seated.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         <a href="/wiki/James_A._Garfield" title="James A. Garfield">\n          James A. Garfield\n         </a>\n         ,\n         <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of Presidents of the United States">\n          President of the United States\n         </a>\n         from Ohio\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       On February 19, 1803, U.S. President\n       <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">\n        Thomas Jefferson\n       </a>\n       signed an act of Congress that approved Ohio\'s boundaries and constitution.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">\n        <a href="#cite_note-66">\n         [66]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       However, Congress had never passed a resolution formally admitting Ohio as the 17th state. The current custom of Congress declaring an official date of statehood did not begin until 1812,\n       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        [\n        <i>\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Disputed statement">\n          <span title="This claim has reliable sources with contradicting facts (September 2017)">\n           disputed\n          </span>\n         </a>\n         <span class="metadata">\n          \xe2\x80\x93\n          <a href="/wiki/Talk:Ohio" title="Talk:Ohio">\n           discuss\n          </a>\n         </span>\n        </i>\n        ]\n       </sup>\n       with\n       <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">\n        Louisiana\n       </a>\n       \'s admission as the 18th state. Although no formal resolution of admission was required, when the oversight was discovered in 1953, Ohio congressman\n       <a href="/wiki/George_H._Bender" title="George H. Bender">\n        George H. Bender\n       </a>\n       introduced a bill in Congress to admit Ohio to the Union retroactive to March 1, 1803, the date on which the\n       <a href="/wiki/Ohio_General_Assembly" title="Ohio General Assembly">\n        Ohio General Assembly\n       </a>\n       first convened.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blue_67-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Blue-67">\n         [67]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       At a special session at the old state capital in\n       <a href="/wiki/Chillicothe,_Ohio" title="Chillicothe, Ohio">\n        Chillicothe\n       </a>\n       , the Ohio state legislature approved a new petition for statehood that was delivered to Washington, D.C. on horseback. On August 7, 1953 (the year of Ohio\'s 150th anniversary),\n       <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">\n        President Eisenhower\n       </a>\n       signed a congressional\n       <a href="/wiki/Joint_resolution" title="Joint resolution">\n        joint resolution\n       </a>\n       that officially declared March 1, 1803, the date of Ohio\'s admittance into the Union.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blue_67-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Blue-67">\n         [67]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">\n        <a href="#cite_note-68">\n         [68]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">\n        <a href="#cite_note-69">\n         [69]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Ohio has had three capital cities: Chillicothe,\n       <a href="/wiki/Zanesville,_Ohio" title="Zanesville, Ohio">\n        Zanesville\n       </a>\n       , and\n       <a href="/wiki/Columbus,_Ohio" title="Columbus, Ohio">\n        Columbus\n       </a>\n       . Chillicothe was the capital from 1803 to 1810. The capital was then moved to Zanesville for two years, as part of a state legislative compromise, in order to get a bill passed. The capital was then moved back to Chillicothe, which was the capital from 1812 to 1816. Finally, the capital was moved to Columbus, in order to have it near the geographic center of the state, where it would be more accessible to most citizens.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Although many Native Americans had migrated west to evade American encroachment, others remained settled in the state, sometimes assimilating in part.\n       <a href="/wiki/Shawnee" title="Shawnee">\n        Shawnee\n       </a>\n       leader\n       <a href="/wiki/Tecumseh" title="Tecumseh">\n        Tecumseh\n       </a>\n       led an\n       <a href="/wiki/Tecumseh%27s_Confederacy" title="Tecumseh\'s Confederacy">\n        American Indian confederacy\n       </a>\n       in\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tecumseh%27s_Rebellion" title="Tecumseh\'s Rebellion">\n        Tecumseh\'s Rebellion\n       </a>\n       , from 1811 to 1813. In 1830 under President\n       <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">\n        Andrew Jackson\n       </a>\n       , the US government forced\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indian_Removal" title="Indian Removal">\n        Indian Removal\n       </a>\n       of most tribes to the\n       <a href="/wiki/Indian_Territory" title="Indian Territory">\n        Indian Territory\n       </a>\n       west of the Mississippi River.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In 1835, Ohio fought with\n       <a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">\n        Michigan\n       </a>\n       in the\n       <a href="/wiki/Toledo_War" title="Toledo War">\n        Toledo War\n       </a>\n       , a mostly bloodless boundary war over the Toledo Strip. Only one person was injured in the conflict. Congress intervened, making Michigan\'s admittance as a state conditional on ending the conflict. In exchange for giving up its claim to the Toledo Strip, Michigan was given the western two-thirds of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Upper_Peninsula_of_Michigan" title="Upper Peninsula of Michigan">\n        Upper Peninsula\n       </a>\n       , in addition to the eastern third that was already considered part of the state.\n      </p>\n      <div class="thumb tleft">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ohio.JPG">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="839" data-file-width="1035" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Ohio.JPG/220px-Ohio.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Ohio.JPG/330px-Ohio.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Ohio.JPG/440px-Ohio.JPG 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ohio.JPG" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Ohio state welcome sign, in an older (1990s) style\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <div class="thumb tleft">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ohio_schild.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="1096" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Ohio_schild.jpg/220px-Ohio_schild.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Ohio_schild.jpg/330px-Ohio_schild.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Ohio_schild.jpg/440px-Ohio_schild.jpg 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ohio_schild.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Newer state sign, (\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Route_52_in_Ohio" title="U.S. Route 52 in Ohio">\n          US\xc2\xa052\n         </a>\n         )\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Ohio\'s central position and its population gave it an important place during the\n       <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">\n        Civil War\n       </a>\n       . The Ohio River was a vital artery for troop and supply movements, as were Ohio\'s railroads. The industry of Ohio made the state one of the most important states in the union during the Civil war. Ohio contributed more soldiers per-capita than any other state in the Union. In 1862, the state\'s morale was badly shaken in the aftermath of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Shiloh" title="Battle of Shiloh">\n        battle of Shiloh\n       </a>\n       , a costly victory in which Ohio forces suffered 2,000 casualties.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-knepper233-234_70-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-knepper233-234-70">\n         [70]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Later that year, when\n       <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_Army" title="Confederate States Army">\n        Confederate\n       </a>\n       troops under the leadership of\n       <a href="/wiki/Stonewall_Jackson" title="Stonewall Jackson">\n        Stonewall Jackson\n       </a>\n       threatened Washington, D.C., Ohio governor\n       <a href="/wiki/David_Tod" title="David Tod">\n        David Tod\n       </a>\n       still could recruit 5,000 volunteers to provide three months of service.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-roseboom188_71-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-roseboom188-71">\n         [71]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       From July 12 to July 23, 1863, Southern Ohio and Indiana were attacked in\n       <a href="/wiki/Morgan%27s_Raid" title="Morgan\'s Raid">\n        Morgan\'s Raid\n       </a>\n       . While this raid was insignificant and small, it aroused fear among people in Ohio and Indiana.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">\n        <a href="#cite_note-72">\n         [72]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Almost 35,000 Ohioans died in the conflict, and 30,000 were physically wounded.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cayton129_73-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-cayton129-73">\n         [73]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       By the end of the Civil War, the Union\'s top three generals\xe2\x80\x93\n       <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant" title="Ulysses S. Grant">\n        Ulysses S. Grant\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_T._Sherman" title="William T. Sherman">\n        William Tecumseh Sherman\n       </a>\n       , and\n       <a href="/wiki/Philip_Sheridan" title="Philip Sheridan">\n        Philip Sheridan\n       </a>\n       \xe2\x80\x93were all from Ohio.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morris10-11_74-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-morris10-11-74">\n         [74]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cayton128-129_75-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-cayton128-129-75">\n         [75]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In 1912 a Constitutional Convention was held with Charles B. Galbreath as secretary. The result reflected the concerns of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Progressive_Era" title="Progressive Era">\n        Progressive Era\n       </a>\n       . It introduced the initiative and the referendum. In addition, it allowed the General Assembly to put questions on the ballot for the people to ratify laws and\n       <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_amendment" title="Constitutional amendment">\n        constitutional amendments\n       </a>\n       originating in the Legislature. Under the Jeffersonian principle that laws should be reviewed once a generation, the constitution provided for a recurring question to appear on Ohio\'s general election ballots every 20 years. The question asks whether a new convention is required. Although the question has appeared in 1932, 1952, 1972, and 1992, it has never been approved. Instead constitutional amendments have been proposed by petition to the legislature hundreds of times and adopted in a majority of cases.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Eight US Presidents hailed from Ohio at the time of their elections, giving rise to its nickname "Mother of Presidents", a sobriquet it shares with\n       <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">\n        Virginia\n       </a>\n       . It is also termed "Modern Mother of Presidents",\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">\n        <a href="#cite_note-76">\n         [76]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       in contrast to Virginia\'s status as the origin of presidents earlier in American history. Seven Presidents were born in Ohio, making it second to Virginia\'s eight. Virginia-born\n       <a href="/wiki/William_Henry_Harrison" title="William Henry Harrison">\n        William Henry Harrison\n       </a>\n       lived most of his life in Ohio and is also buried there. Harrison conducted his political career while living on the family compound, founded by his father-in-law,\n       <a href="/wiki/John_Cleves_Symmes" title="John Cleves Symmes">\n        John Cleves Symmes\n       </a>\n       , in\n       <a href="/wiki/North_Bend,_Ohio" title="North Bend, Ohio">\n        North Bend, Ohio\n       </a>\n       . The seven presidents born in Ohio were\n       <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant" title="Ulysses S. Grant">\n        Ulysses S. Grant\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Rutherford_B._Hayes" title="Rutherford B. Hayes">\n        Rutherford B. Hayes\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/James_A._Garfield" title="James A. Garfield">\n        James A. Garfield\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Harrison" title="Benjamin Harrison">\n        Benjamin Harrison\n       </a>\n       (grandson of William Henry Harrison),\n       <a href="/wiki/William_McKinley" title="William McKinley">\n        William McKinley\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/William_Howard_Taft" title="William Howard Taft">\n        William Howard Taft\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Warren_G._Harding" title="Warren G. Harding">\n        Warren G. Harding\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">\n        Demographics\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <table class="toccolours" style="border-spacing: 1px;
